===== PAGE 12 =====
ROSH HASHANAH 5725
APPLES AND HONEY
A very nice trait of Jewish people is their asso¬
ciation of food values with religious values. While
rabbis decry the type of Jewish identification that
places timely appetizing indulgences over timeless
authentic observances, there is no denying that
the spirits of Passover and Shavuot, Hanukkah
and Purim are warmed and enriched by designed
dietary delights. Not merely as an accedence to
human nature, but more as a positive means of
materializing tangibly the meaning and ideals of
a religious celebration, this practice has been en¬
couraged and preserved.
The Jewish New Year, too, has its symbolic
foods. Considering in fact, that Rosh Hashanah
is pre-eminently a synagogue centered occasion,
the dietary customs provide the major accent on
home observance. The usual braided challah is
replaced by one that is perfectly round, symbolizing
the fervent wish that life in the coming year will
be smooth and whole. An apple is sliced, covered
with honey, and eaten, after imploring God's will
for a sweet year.
It is the latter custom that arouses my curiosity.
The honey is obviously symbolic. Why not eat a
spoonful or a few drops of honey? Why the apple?
The apple is often tart; it has a peel which is hard
to chew, and seeds and core which are totally un¬
palatable. Whole, it may be ruddy and beautiful,
inside it may be spoiled. It is at best, partly inedible.
It is suggestive of the new year, and the reality
of life.
RABBI ARNOLD S. TURETSKY
On the outside it appears promising; inside is
mystery and uncertainty. At the very best it will
have parts that will be hard to chew, harder to
swallow. It includes sections that cannot be ingested,
they must be separated and discarded. On this
material, sometimes sour, we place a little honey.
This is the sweetness of one’s work, which is some¬
times fulfilling. It is the pleasantness of family joys,
the soothing of love, the comfort of friendship,
the inspiration of a symphony, the thrill of a book.
It does not materially change the produce of one’s
life, but it leaves a lingering taste of sweetness.
This is the best we can hope for. A mouthful
of honey would leave our taste jaded and insipid.
It is an impossible diet. The apple makes it real.
To you and all your loved ones from our house
to yours, Shanah Tovah Umetukah — a healthy,
happy and sweet New Year.
Rabbi Arnold S. Turetsky

An open letter to the membership:
The Center needs the services of all of its members
to serve on committees and become an active part of the
synagogue team.
In our synagogue we have willing workers, those
who are willing to work and others who are willing to let
them.
Why not pitch in and join the committee of your
choice to further enrich the highly diversified Center
program
Compliments of
Louis Portnoy — Walter Portnoy

===== PAGE 14 =====
Synagogue—Center Administration
Mr. Bowden
Comptroller
Mrs. Bernard Stegman
Secretary
Robert Fischer
Executive Director
Mrs. Florence Robin
Secretary
Mrs. Esther Fishier
Secretary
As one of the outstanding Synagogue-Centers in the
Southeast, we offer our membership a dynamic and di¬
versified program in every phase of religious, educational,
social, cultural and athletic activity. In glancing through
this book a brief summary in pictures and words depicts
our year-round activity with a bird’s-eye view of our
over-all program. Your comments concerning our opera¬
tion and your continued support are always solicited.
Special thanks are due to our office personnel in help¬
ing to carry out the many details of your Synagogue-
Center administration. My personal appreciation to Mrs.
Ann Stegman, Mrs. Florence Robin, Mrs. Esther Fishier
and Mr. Kenneth Bowden for their devoted and untiring
efforts on behalf of our synagogue. At this time I would
also like to welcome Mr. Richard N. Pokrass who has
recently joined our staff in the supervisory capacity of
administering our weddings. It is with a great deal of
pleasure that we look forward to a long and lasting
association.
On behalf of the entire administration, maintenance
and transportation staff we take this opportunity of ex¬
tending to our entire membership a Happy and Pros¬
perous New Year.
Robert Fischer, Executive Director
Mr. Richard Pokrass
Wedding Consultant
Brief Scope of Activities
Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are
held daily, morning and evening in the Chapel. Friday
Family Services, Sabbath and Holiday Services are ob¬
served in the Sanctuary.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish
customs are observed in connection with Circumcision,
naming of girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat
Mitzvah, Confirmations, Marriages, Conversion to Juda¬
ism, Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten with outstanding
program of secular and religious content. Hebrew School,
carefully graded with intensive Hebrew content. Sunday
School, with a curriculum including Jewish history and
customs. Foundation School, a graded secular and He¬
brew Day School, 1st through the 3rd grade.
Supplementary religious educational training is prac¬
ticed in Pre-Jr., Jr. and Teen Congregation services and
Minyan Clubs.
Extension Services — Library — outstanding collec¬
tion of juvenile and adult Jewish books. Adult Education
courses on religious and cultural Jewish subjects.
Music — a program of religious and cultural Jewish
music. Sisterhood and Men’s Club render valuable serv¬
ice to the Center and its members. Social Activities pro¬
vided by Mr. and Mrs. Club, Amity Club and “32
Club”. Family Recreation Program, summer week-days
and Sundays for all ages at our Camp Site.
Youth Activities — United Synagogue Youth,
various age levels are served by Center Youth League,
Charles Palot Club, Major's Pre-Teens, Tween-Agers.
Scouting — Boy Scouts, Cub Scouts, Brownies and Girl
Scouts. Athletics — programs for youth and adults.
Day Camp — A nine week summer program at our new
Camp Site.

A dm in istra tion
Transportation
Left to right: Our station wagon used for small pick-ups with our three 66 passenger buses
and the drivers.
To keep our transportation at its peak performance and maintain our outstanding safety record we have Morris
Montford who has been with us for the past twenty years, Eugene Davis a 14 year veteran behind the wheel, with
newcomer W. Henry Mathews. This devoted trio of bus drivers covers the city eight times daily for Kindergarten,
Hebrew School and Foundation School pick-ups during the winter and provides Day Camp transportation during
the summer months. The entire transportation system of routes and maintenance is under the supervision of our
Comptroller, Kenneth Bowden, and the chairman of the Transportation Committee, Mr. Herbert Lewitt.
Maintenance
Our loyal maintenance crew composed of Tim Riley,
Wallace Mazon and Frank Edwards are responsible for
the constant upkeep and set-up of our Synagogue-
Center. These veterans of many years have devoted
countless hours to seeing that all functions are prepared
for in the best possible manner.
Kitchen
A familiar figure in the Kitchen at all Center dinners
and banquets is Annie Mae Fort, our kitchen supervisor
for the past 18 years.
Providing the tasty food at most Center affairs and
members' Simhas are our two caterers Mrs. Mary Safer
and the Meyerhoffs whose meals speak for themselves.
On behalf of the Board of Directors, Officers and myself, we would like to take this opportunity to pay special
tribute to this loyal and devoted transportation, maintenance and kitchen staff who have given unselfishly of them¬
selves during the past twenty years.

===== PAGE 18 =====
!
■ ■
Much has been said and even more has been written during the
past year as to the future of American Judaism. There are those who
are convinced that the abandonment of Judaism by the majority of
Jews in America is inevitable. There are others who are just as certain
that a golden era of creative religious achievement lies ahead.
This divergence of opinion is understandable since there is abun¬
dant evidence that both views may be correct. On the one hand there
are large segments of the Jewish community which are moving towards
extinction as Jews. Yet there are other groups which are just as
definitely moving to higher standards of Jewish living with the con¬
fidence that Judaism will not only survive but that it will flourish.
Our own synagogue can present many achievements which would
show why this confidence is warranted. The Sabbath morning attend¬
ance of both adults and children is improving. The number enrolled in
adult education continues to grow. Enrollment in the Hebrew High
School has increased, while the number of children attending Camp
Ramah has doubled and re-doubled in the last few years. The age of
Confirmation has been pushed to within one year of high school gradu¬
ation. A third grade has been added to our Day School. The daily
Minyan has been strengthened by the support of a strong nucleus of
young persons. Many additional achievements could be cited.
Much of the satisfaction from these achievements is dulled by the
thought that these successes have not involved the masses to any great
extent. One must admit that should the present patterns of Jewish
behaviour of the majority of our own synagogue continue, their ulti¬
mate abandonment of Judaism is inevitable. There are some who are
convinced that there is very little which can be done to arrest the
movement of the masses towards their extinction as Jews. Certainly
there is no challenge for those who are of this conviction.
PHILIP SELBER, President of the Congregation
The challenge, however, is great for those who
are convinced that history confirms their confi¬
dence in the resourcefulness and inner strength of
Judaism to retain its masses regardless of the
strength of the forces opposing it. What then is
the role of the conservative synagogue in meeting
this challenge? It must insist that its members be¬
come committed to the proposition that there are
certain minimum standards of conduct and ob¬
servance without which it is impossible for the
individual Jew to retain his identity with Judaism.
BURTON I. GROSSMAN
Chairman of the
Board of Directors
Past President
Adherence to standards is especially difficult for the conservative
synagogue, because it not only believes in retaining the standards in¬
herited from the past, but because it aligns itself with the forces of
historic Judaism in holding that these standards must constantly
be reviewed and adjusted to make possible and meaningful the day
to day living of life according to the Torah. Thus, when the conserva¬
tive synagogue gives credence to the experiences of the past or intensi¬
fies its efforts for greater adherence to standards as expounded by the
authoritative body within the conservative movement, it runs the
danger of being labeled orthodox. When it advocates change in prac¬
tices, it is exposed to an equally unjust charge that it is tending
towards reform.
As difficult as the task may be, we must not deviate from our basic
commitment both to tradition and to its change. Let us hope that our
synagogue will not be deterred from its determination to follow the
path of historic Judaism and that with the help of God it can succeed
in building a religiously committed membership forever linked with
the eternal ideals and aspirations of Judaism.
On behalf of the Officers and members of the Board of Directors
and the Board of Trustees, may we extend a greeting to all of our
members for a Happy New Year, in the traditional words of
“Leshanah Tovah Tikatevu”.

you experience Central’s "All Vice-Presidential” kind of banking. The

Board Chairman Alvin Leitman). You see, everyone at Central is a vice
president—a rather special kind, in charge of helping people. So you get
1st Class officer-type treatment from our doorman on up.

===== PAGE 32 =====
Building Fund Committee
LOUIS PORTNOY
Co-Chairman
LOUIS SAFER WILBUR MARGOL
Co-Chairman Treasurer
Scenes at our new Olympic Pool, depicting
a swimming class and diving class.
This past year saw the culmination of several years of
planning when the Center acquired a 36 acre site in the
Southside, fronting on State Road 13. The site was im¬
mediately improved with facilities for recreational activities,
including an Olympic size swimming pool and bath house,
two handball courts, tennis-basketball court, volley ball
court, two baseball fields, archery range, and children’s play
area. Improvements were also made to the 13 room, two
story house that was on the property. Many additional im¬
provements are being planned by the committee, to be made
each year as financing permits. These will include beautifica¬
tion of the site and front entrance with shrubbery, trees and
grass, construction of entrance sign, paving of the entrance
road and parking area, providing shade areas at swimming
pool.
The site plan of the property provides for future con¬
struction of a synagogue, educational building, chapel, audi¬
torium and other facilities.
The Building Fund Com¬
mittee has also continued its
responsibility for maintaining
the existing buildings at Third
and Silver Streets in excellent
condition and in meeting mort¬
gage payments.
The congregation’s con¬
tinued support of the Building
Fund Committee is vital to our
successful synagogue program.
Camp Site Committee
To meet the many demands at the new Camp Site and
to constantly improve the facilities the following committee
under the chairmanship of Irwin Gendzier and Sidney
Berkowitz was appointed to work on a year round basis to
make our future home a relaxing experience in the outdoors
during the summer months. This committee also has the
responsibility of maintaining this property during the winter
as well as the summer so that our Day Camp and Family
Recreation Program may have the best physical plant at all
times.

Center Choir and Glee Club
Pictured on this page is the Center Choir and Clee Club under the
capable direction of Cantor Abraham Marton who for the past 16 years
has presented the annual Concert of the Choral Society, a 30 voice adult
choir, augmented by the children's Glee Club of 70 voices. This past year’s
Concert held on March 8 highlighted a Friday Evening Service with
Samuel Sherter as soloist with the secular part of the program highlighting
Nelle Watkins, Martha Tanner and Edward Doe as soloists.
Proceeds of the Concert are annually donated to the Cantor's Institute
of the Jewish Theological Seminary of America.

Sisterhood In Action
1964 Donors. Left to right, Mrs. Louis Slott, Sisterhood Presi¬
dent, Mrs. Robert Fischer, Co-Chairman of Donors, presenting
Donors check to Byron Morris, Chairman of the Day Camp
Committee.
Gift Shop Co-Chairmen, Mrs. Ben Ed¬
wards and Mrs. I. Gartner, behind gift
counter in Synagogue Lobby.
The year 1963-64 was started with a delightful opening
luncheon titled “Hats Off To Noemie”, honoring Mrs.
Arnold Turetsky and introducing her to our membership.
The October meeting was a huge success with a guest
speaker, Mrs. Mitzi Mann, from Miami who spoke on the
Combined Campaign. Mrs. Louis Moss gave a large contri¬
bution and was our first Cha’yay Olam Contributor.
The annual Membership Tea was held in the evening
and together with our husbands, we enjoyed the magnificent
program ‘'Jewish Home Beautiful”. The beautiful holiday
tables were set up traditionally and presented by members
of the Sisterhood in program form.
The Chanukah Fair was a huge success and was attended
by children, parents and grandparents. Food, games and
white elephants were available to all. As exciting as the year
before was the Art Exhibit chaired by Mrs. William H.
Schemer and Mrs. Harry Yoffee featuring original paintings
and crafts by Sisterhood and Center Members.
An Oneg Shabbat was held after services January 11th.
Brief book reviews were presented by Mrs. Lewis Ansbacher,
Mrs. Harold Newman, Miss Minnie Schreiber and Mrs.
Bernard Meyer.
In February, the Paid-Up Donor Affair program was
“I Remember Daughter” starring Mrs. Samuel Thier, a past
President, and a cast of many of our lovely members and
their children who modeled beautifully.
(Continued on Page 39)
Mrs. Ted Gross, Mrs. Norman Moss and Mrs. Philip
Selber, displaying the Sisterhood’s World Book project and
its many advantages for the children.
Torah Fund contributors from left to right: Mrs. Sheldon
Gendzier, Chairman; guest speaker, Mrs. Milton Mann,
and Mrs. Louis Moss.

Sisterhood Calendar For 1964-1965
“AJol what you receive, but what you give, measures the worth of the
life you live”.
October 7. FASHION SHOW—MEMBERSHIP TEA
November 4. A NARRATIVE WRITTEN BY RABBI
ARNOLD TURETSKY featuring Jewish Book
Month—Joint dinner meeting with the Men's
Club.
November 29. HANUKAH FAIR
December 2. KICK-OFF BRUNCH FOR DONORS
January 6. SOCIAL ACTIONS—National Women's
League Anniversary
Sisterhood in Action
(Continued from Page 37)
The Torah Fund Program was an inspiration to all
making us aware of our responsibilities as Jewish women.
This year, we again had "‘Breakfast with the Rebetzin”, a
brunch held at the home of Rabbi and Mrs. Arnold Turetsky
honoring our Torah Fund Contributors. "Phis fund helps to
maintain the Seminary and its many projects.
April 1st was a very interesting Social Action program
with Mr. Bill Basford, State Representative, as our guest
speaker.
The Closing Luncheon was held on May 6th featuring
the Installation of New Officers. Mrs. Jack Becker and Mrs.
Nat Cohen were the narrators of this most inspiring candle-
lighting ceremony. Afterwards, Mrs. Norman Slaff, the
newly installed President presented Mrs. Louis Slott, out¬
going President, with a gift from the Sisterhood in apprecia¬
tion of her leadership and devotion. Rabbi Arnold Turetsky
added to the Installation with a magnificent message.
February 3. CANTATA (Honoring Paid-up Donors)
February 20. ONEG SHABBAT (Kiddush Luncheon
after Services)
March 3. PURIMROMA—Token Luncheon
March 17. PURIM SUPPER
April 7. COMBINED CAMPAIGN FOR TORAH
FUND AND THE MATHILDE SCHECTER
RESIDENCE HALL
May 5. CLOSING LUNCHEON featuring Mother’s Day
Sisterhood works very closely with our two United
Synagogue Youth groups and sponsors the Girl Scouts and
Brownie Troops. This year, we are happy to announce that
we presented four scholarships for our youth to attend Camp
Ramah.
The annual Florida Branch Conference was held in
Hollywood Beach on May 11th, 12th, and 13th, and was
attended by Mrs. Arnold Turetsky, Mrs. Mitchell Peltz, Mrs.
Norman Slaff and Mrs. Beryl Weinstein. We are very fortu¬
nate that the 1965 Conference will be here in Jacksonville
on May 10, 11, & 12.
Funds which enable us to fulfill our obligations to the
Center are obtained from our Gift Shop, sale of Barton’s
Candy, World Book Encyclopedia, Leagrams, and the
beautiful family tablecloth put together by Mrs. Harry
Slott and embroidered by many of our members.
We are looking forward to another successful year with
your continued support. We, of Sisterhood, wish you and
your dear ones a very wonderful year.

The Center Men’s Club
This year the Center Men's Club has been in a whirl¬
wind of activities, which only could have been undertaken
by the many capable and dedicated young members. Credit
for the success of the meetings goes mainly to Gerald Sohn,
Chairman of programs, which ranged from joint meetings
with the Etz Chaim and the Temple Men’s Club to an
enlightening discussion with two gubernatorial candidates.
Helping to make the monthly dinner meetings successful
was Dave Safer, Food Chairman, whose efforts in making
our meals as delicious as always helped to bolster our
attendance.
Stanley Vandroff made the Annual Membership Affair,
which was attended by more than two hundred couples, a
tremendous success with everyone looking forward to
another gala event in 1965.
Over $1,000 was raised at the Men's Club Picnic, which
was held in conjunction with the informal Dedication of the
Center's new property. Special thanks for the success of the
annual Sunday School Picnic goes to Milton Felder and
Robert Shmunes, Stanley Kantor, Buddy Shorstein, Morton
Harris, Dave Safer, Melvin Freidlin, Jack Shorstein, Neil
Presser and Robert Fischer. These funds helped sponsor
several scholarships towards Camp Ramah which is an
integral part of our Religious School training.
In addition to the monthly affairs, the Men's Club is
always engaged in various other programs. Scholarships for
Camp Ramah and Camp Philmont, a Blood Bank under
the direction of Herbert Lewitt and Melvin Esman, an
STANLEY KANTOR,
President
adult education program, and Minyan breakfasts under the
capable supervision of Morton Harris and Bernard Heyman
are some of the activities engaged in by members of the
Men's Club.
Plans for next year are already under way to boost the
membership and all members, old and new, urged to partici¬
pate on any committee of their choice.

Dinner Meeting.
At Left, Louis Slott spin¬
ning the wheel of fortune
at the Annual Sunday School
Picnic in the Recreation Hall
of the New Camp Site.

1964 Ramah Campers
(Not Pictured—Ned Price and Davida Weiss)
The great demand for attendance at Camp Ramah can be witnessed by the
24 students of our Religious Schools who attended this eight week Hebrew oriented
camp sponsored by the United Synagogue through its Commission on Camp Ramah.
Representing the Jacksonville Jewish Center at Camp Ramah from all grade levels
represents a 100% increase over our 1963 delegation and the largest contingent
from any synagogue in the Southeast. Our congregation can well be proud of this
achievement and step in the right direction.

Now in its 5th year of operation, the Tofield Memorial
Library under the capable and loyal direction of Mrs. Philip
Selber and Librarians Mrs. Bernard Glossman and Mrs.
Archie Boatwright has shown great strides and appreciable
increase in its selection of reading material.
Always expanding their services and hours of operation
the Library is now open each Sunday morning and with the
cooperation and assistance of the Sisterhood Library Com¬
mittee under the chairmanship of Mrs. Byron Morris has
made it possible for the Library to be open Monday through
Thursday from 3 to 5 P.M. In addition to the above hours
the Library is also available in conjunction with the Adult
Education classes.

===== PAGE 68 =====
TED GROSS, Principal
Religious Education
Those who have been following our program by reading the annual reports
must be impressed by the remarkable innovations that the school sponsors each
year.
The highlights of the past year s activities are led by the vast expansion
in the Ramah enrollment for the summer of 1964. When parents are willing to
send 22 children to a summer camp which is one thousand miles away from
home, they must consider that the advantages to be gained from this intensive
program are worth the additional effort.
Secondly, meaningful education must use developed skills in a continuous
fashion. The introduction of a Post-Bar Mitzvah Torah Reading class for the
main synagogue service will ensure the continuation of skills developed before
Bar Mitzvah.
i \
Intensive education is again reflected in the arrangements to open the third
V.
grade of Foundation School for the next school year. This department will now
have its own supervisor.
This has been the first year that the 12th grade students have had regular
classes in Hebrew High School, and the first year when a number of graduates
have been able to conduct classes in Hebrew School on their own. It has also
been the first year that the Sunday School Confirmation was celebrated at the
end of the 11th grade.
In-service courses, taught during the month of June, for our Sunday School
staff is the beginning of teaching in depth that has become the mark of our
educational program.
Through the development of local teachers of Hebrew, all Sunday School
students are being required to possess a knowledge of Hebrew prayers and
worship before being confirmed.
These activities have been made possible by the dedicated efforts of the
members of the staff, the Education Committee, Men’s Club, Sisterhood, and
the special committees for particular occasions.
The school curriculum will be even more intensified in the coming season,
following the announcement passed by the Center Board to the effect that the
third grade of Sunday Religious School be co-ordinated with Hebrew School
classes to provide an integrated six-hours-a-aweek course of instruction.
DR. NATHAN
SCHNEIDER
Education Chairman
MRS. HANA

Our Achievements
The goal of the Jacksonville Jewish Center school is to
have the highest standard in the country. The accomplish¬
ments achieved during the past year include the following:
• Continuous education from 3 to 18 years of age.
• Advanced departments in Hebrew School and
Sunday School.
• A Foundation School of First, Second Grades and
opening of Third Grade.

Hebrew School
Goals . . .
The progress of any type of school program is usually
measured by two essentials: a high goal showing that the
school is progressive and never static, and progressive innova¬
tions to show how this high goal is being achieved.
It is the goal of the Jacksonville Jewish Center educational
program to have the highest standard possible with the use of
the latest possible teaching methods.
Those who have followed the program's development
during the recent year must be impressed by the remarkable
innovations that the school has sponsored.
The highlights of the past year's activities are led by the
vast expansion in the Camp Ramah enrollment for the sum-
Ramah Teaching Staff: Rose Raskin, Linda Hal-
pern, Deborah Lederman, Richard Browdy,
Sheila Fages, Howard Rosenblatt and Michael
Schneider.
1964 Hebrew High School Graduates with
Principal Ted Gross: Front row, Jack Snider,
Gail Price, Jack Mizrahi & Leonard Selber.
Second row, Michael Wilensky, Dorothy Green-
baum & Jerry Zucker.
mer of 1964. This year 22 children will be sent over one
thousand miles away to gain the advantages from this inten¬
sive program.
Secondly, meaningful education must use developed skills
in a continuous fashion. The introduction of a Post-Bar
Mitzvah Torah Reading class for the main synagogue service
will ensure the continuation of skills developed before Bar
Mitzvah.
Intensive education is again reflected in the arrangements
to open the third grade of the Foundation School for the
next school year, and this department will now have its
own supervisor.

Daily Kindergarten
Our Daily Kindergarten and Nursery now in its
24th year of operation is among the best in the city.
Our outstanding staff is composed of Mrs. M. J. Reiser,
who had the first graduating class in 1942, and the
veteran teachers who have been with us more than ten
years, Mrs. Carl Hackel, instructor of the oldest group,
Mrs. Harry Jaffe, instructor of four year olds and Mrs.
Edward Sussman, teacher of the youngest group. The
program and curriculum are under the direction of
Ted Gross, principal.

===== PAGE 100 =====
Youth Activities
A strong congregation and synagogue in the future is built
through spiritual and instructive training of the youth of today.
At the Center a great deal of emphasis is placed on the proper
training and direction of the current youth in the congregation today,
and for that reason there are Center youth clubs and organizations
for children of every age level.
Serving as Director of the Youth Activities program to set the
policy for the many organizations is Robert Fischer.
Dr. Jack Rosner is Chairman of the Center Youth Commission
and in that capacity he coordinates all youth activities programs and
sets the standards for future goals. Coordinating the youth activities for
the Sisterhood is Mrs. Leon Schinasi, which organization helps to
sponsor and finance part of our youth program.
Youthful Stanley Kantor serves as Chairman of the USY program
in which he takes charge over the two U.S.Y. teenage groups, C.Y.L.
and C.P.C., which are affiliated with United Synagogue Youth as
mentioned above.
Under the direction of such capable advisors as Kantor, Buddy
Shorstein, Evelyn Brauner, Ada Hinda Block and Enid Goldberg
these two groups have rapidly gained recognition as the most in¬
fluential chapters in the Southeast Region.
Center Youth Organizations
Robert Fischer
Youth Activities Director
Dr. Jack Rosner
Youth Commission
Chairman
Center Youth League (CYL)—A co-ed U.S.Y. Chapter for 11th
and 12th graders, meeting every 2nd and 4th Sunday with varied
programs from September to May.
Charles Palot Club (CPC)—U.S.Y. Chapter for 9th and 10th grade
boys and girls, meeting throughout the school year on the 2nd
and 4th Sunday at the Center.
Major’s Pre-Teens—A co-ed youth club for 7th and 8th graders
with its programs geared to this pre-teen level, with varied social,
religious and athletic functions held monthly.
Tween-Agers—Boys and girls of the 5th and 6th grades participate
monthly in this, our youngest club.
To this list is also added our sponsorship of Boy Scouts (Troop 14),
Cub Scouts (Pack 14), Girl Scouts (Troops 6 and 193) and
Brownie Troops (210 and 269).
Stanley Kantor
USY Chairman
USY Advisory Board
U.S.Y. Advisory Board (L to R) Ada Hinda Block-CYL Advisor, Enid Goldberg-CPC Advisor, Evelyn Brauner-CYL Advisor,
(2nd row) Stanley Kantor-USY Chairman, Robert Fischer-Director of Youth Activities, and S. Robert Shorstein-CYL Advisor.

===== PAGE 102 =====
United Synagogue Youth
Center Youth League
G.Y.L. (Center Youth League) is an organization
affiliated with United Synagogue Youth, whose main
interests are closely associated with the Synagogue plus
a varied program of cultural, social, and athletic activi¬
ties.
The total aims of the USY program itself may be
summed up in the words ‘"Bring the Youth Back to the
Synagogue/'
C.Y.L. had a very successful year during the past
season under the guidance and direction of its two very
competent advisors, Buddy Shorstein and Evelyn
Brauner. The year was highlighted by the first BSB
National Send-off which received regional and national
recognition. The ruach built up at this Kinus Katon
provided a real incentive for an inspirational as well as
enjoyable National Convention.
At our National Kinus this past year in New York
at the Hotel Statler-Hilton, C.Y.L. received a chapter
award for programming, five individual membership
awards as well as a BSB Fund Raising award for one
of the largest contributions in the nation. Along with
its national recognition, C.Y.L. had a number of com¬
munity service projects which included a play for the
residents of the River Garden Hebrew Home for the
Aged.
Advisors for 1964-65 will be: S. Robert Shorstein
and Miss Rita Weinstein.
Linda Halpern, 1962-63 Recipient of the Best USY’er
Award presenting the same award for 1963-64 to Richard
Browdy with Karen Caslar receiving the Best Girl Award for
1963-64 from Robert Fischer.
1964-65 OFFICERS: Left to Right, Evelyn Brauner, Ad¬
visor; Sheila Fages, Rec.-Sec.; Phyllis Glossman, Corr.-Sec.;
Sheila Kramer, Vice-Pres.; Judy Horovitz, Vice-Pres.; 2nd
Row: Leonard Selber, Vice-Pres.; Jack Mizrahi, Treasurer;
Chuck Slott, President; S. Robert Shorstein, Advisor.
All in all a very worthwhile and enjoyable year was
had by all. C.Y.L. cordially invites all potential mem¬
bers between the ages of 15-17 to participate with them
this coming year and help them to grow even stronger,
larger, and more dedicated to the synagogue.

United Synagogue Youth
Charles Palot Club
1964-65
OFFICERS
President „ Mendy Marks
Vice-President Gilda Goldman
Vice-President Dolly Fleet
Vice-President Hilda Goldman
Recording Secretary Michelle Yergin
Corresponding Secretary Debbie Morris
Treasurer Rodney Margol
Advisors Miss Evelyn Brauner and
Miss Enid Goldberg
Another banner year in the annals of the C.P.C.
group has been successfully recorded.
Among the many outstanding and interesting pro¬
grams that were held during the past year were a discus¬
sion by the Director of the Civic Council on Youth Af¬
fairs concerning the council’s teenage activities, and a
fascinating discussion and demonstration on the methods
of hypnotism.
Interesting telephone calls were placed to our former
Rabbi Goldblum in Pittsburg; Carol Silvers, Correspond¬
ing Secretary of USY in New York; and to Dr. Morton
Siegel, Director of the USY.
1964-65 C.P.C. Officers
Conventions were numerous for our local chapter this
year, as we had the honor of serving as the host chapter
for the 400 members in attendance of the Regional Con¬
vention. This was the 13th annual Regional, and a good
and profitable time was enjoyed by all. We also hosted
the first USY National Send-off, to raise money for the
project “Building Spiritual Bridges” which builds dormi¬
tories for schools in Israel. Over $240 was raised for the
fund.
The following represented us at the 1964 L.T.I.:
Saralee Newman, Mendy Marks, Leslie Edwards, and
Dolly Fleet.
Scene from Annual USY Banquet held in June. Both USY Chapers hold this annual dinner-dance at which time officers

Major’s Pre-Teens
With the revision to include two grades in the
Major's Pre-Teens, this group of 7th and 8th grade boys
and girls now are functioning with monthly activities
throughout the school year. Programs are geared to this
pre-teen level through their monthly social, civic and
athletic programs.
The meetings are held immediately following Sunday
School with varied programs from month to month.
Special thanks are due to Mrs. M. J. Reiser who co¬
ordinates the group and the mothers' committee com¬
posed of Mesdames Marvin Ganson, Irwin Gendzier,
Manuel Katz, David Paikin and Isidore Weiss. Appre¬
ciations are also due to the CYL sponsors Melvin
Gottlieb and Anita Mesnick.

Boy Scout Troop 14
Boy Scout Troop 14 under the continued and dedi¬
cated leadership of the Scoutmaster, Dr. Harry Yergin,
is currently among the top troops in the Mohawk District
and always continues to strive for higher goals.
Camping trips were made to Camp Echokotee, Ross
Allen’s at Silver Springs, the national Jamboree and other
weekend retreats whenever school vacations permitted.
Chairman of the Scout Committee, Henry Lasris, along
with 14 Scouts from our Troop spent an entire week at
the summer camp at Echokotee, living in bivouacs
throughout the week. Special thanks are due to Mr. Lasris
for his efforts.
Special mention should also be made of the two
Scouts Ira Price and Richard Darlow representing Troop
14 at the International Boy Scout Jamboree in Valley
Forge, Pa. with over 52,000 Boy Scouts in attendance.
Our annual Blue and Gold Banquet held jointly with
the Cub Scouts had over 250 parents and friends of
Scouting participate in the closing event of our Scout
Weekend.

Cub Scouts Pack 14
The enthusiasm and devotion shown by our Den Mothers and Cub Scouts
has lifted our Pack among the leaders in the city. The 38 Cubs between the ages
of 8 through 10 participate in weekly Den meetings at the respective Den
Mothers' homes with monthly Pack meetings held on the last Thursday of the
month at the Center. Special mention should be made of our first Webelos
Den under the direction of Harry Kowitz, assistant Cubmaster, and to Cub-
master Sidney Engelhardt for his interesting programs at the monthly Pack
meetings. Our sincere thanks to the Pack Committee and the Den Mothers for
a yeoman's job in Cubbing.
Cmdr. Sidney Englhardt

Girl Scouts and Brownies
The Center's two Girl Scout Troops and two Brownie Troops under the
past leadership of Mrs. Leon Schinasi has grown in stature and will be
ably continued under the newly elected Chairman for 1964-65, Mrs.
Howard Hecht. Special thanks are due to the Troop Leaders and Co-
Leaders shown with their Troops below.
The Center Girl Scout program is sponsored and coordinated by the
Sisterhood Vice-President in charge of youth.
The annual closing Banquet in June marks the highlight of the year’s
activities for these girls ages 8 through 13. The girls meet weekly at the
Leaders' homes located in each section of the city.
With this year's completion of the Center Camp Site, these groups look
forward to increased activities made possible by this new facility on the
Southside.

1964 Camp Yom Tov—Day Camp
Day Camp Staff: Bottom row, left to right: Mrs. M. J. Reiser, Lee Cone, Arlene Rosenberg, Etta Sue Fialkow, Elaine
Dittman, Barbara Goldstein, Gail Grossman, Lane Berkwit, Karen Caslar, Rozie Goldstein, Anita Mesnick, Sheila Kramer.
Second row: Jerry Cohn, Avery Weiss, Marvin Slott, Bobby Tucker, Sammy Lasris, Elliot Baker, Mendy Marks, Susan
Stillman, Steve Rosner, Pam Reich, Ellen Grauer and Dolly Fleet. Top row: Robert Fischer, Director; Judy Llorovitz,
Benjie Felder, Bruce Yergin, Jack Fleet, Richard Browdy, Martin Edwards, Stephen Entman, Mrs. Robert Fischer, Assistant
Director, Richard Kidder, Charles Berdit, Steve Margol, Ricky Kraemer, Jason Solomon and Larry Click.
Day Camp Committee Co-Chairmen
Byron Morris and Robert Lasris
Our 6th successful summer under the direction of
Robert Fischer concluded with increased enthusiasm dis¬
played by campers and counselors alike who engaged in
an outstanding program which featured the use of our
new and beautiful 36 acre Camp Site.
The baseball program which featured three teams in
City League competition, CIT program, paddle ball
participation, swimming in the new Olympic sized pool,
and Color War among numerous other activities were the
highlights instituted under our current leadership.
The enrollment included boys and girls 4 through
14 years of age with over 380 youngsters taking advantage
of our nine week summer camp.
Now after 18 years of using rented facilities, the
Camp is proud to have the beautiful new site located on
State Road 13 which will serve as the permanent location
for all our future seasons.
Our entire camp pictured on the basketball court at our new Camp Site.
220 campers and 39 staff members.

===== PAGE 120 =====
Athletics
“A Short Glimpse of the Greenies”
During the short span of just five years Coach Bob Fischer has
built a teenage basketball program that the entire Center can be
proud of.
The four “Greenie” basketball teams have combined to win 53
games while losing only 9, one of the best independent team records
in the city.
In its initial season the senior-led team posted a record of 7
victories against 3 defeats. Steve Moss along with Ed and Bruce
Witten paced the team.
The next team was the center’s best, as it won 16 and lost only
1. Martin Edwards, Rick Stromberg, and Charlie Gusky were the
team leaders.
The ’61 -’62 Center Greenie squad posted a fine mark of 14-5
and featured a spirited guard combination of Jerry Cohn, Jack
Fleet, and forward Steve Strumlauf.
Last year’s undefeated team was led by Mike Roth and Ben
Michaelson. The record was 16-0.
This season the Greenies did not participate as a teenage team,
however Coach Fischer assures us they will be back on the court
again during the upcoming campaign. — Martin Edwards

Mr. and Mrs. Club
OFFICERS
President Mr. and Mrs. Albert Mattes
1st Vice-Pres Mrs. and Mrs. Gus Gross
2nd Vice-Pres Mr. and Mrs. Carl Miller
Treasurer Mr. and Mrs. Stanley Harris
Recording Secretary Mr. and Mrs. Irving Sutton
Corresponding Secretary Mr. and Mrs. Irving Resnick
MEMBERS OF THE BOARD: Mr. and Mrs. Joseph Glick,
Mr. and Mrs. Carl Goldberg, Mr. and Mrs. Mark Miller,
Mr. and Mrs. Ben Dwoskin, Mr. and Mrs. Israel Schemer,
Mr. and Mrs. H. T. Freedman.
The Mr. and Mrs. Club, a social organization now in
its 11th year, meets at the Center for its social and busi¬
ness activities. The Club which limits its membership,
fosters congeniality and good fellowship among its loyal
membership, who form monthly committees to conduct
the dinner and dance affairs each month.
The committees vie with each other in preparing food,
entertainment and decorations in order to earn an ‘Ttzik”
award in the various categories. A family picnic affair is held
in the summer months, an Installation Banquet mid-year
and a New Year’s Eve Party highlight the social whirl
conducted by the Club.
Each member is expected to serve at least once a year on
a monthly committee. Membership in the Club is by ap¬
plication and those interested may contact any of the
Officers or Board members.

The Amity Club
Officers of Amity Club
OFFICERS
President Robert Kauss

Recording Secretary Mrs. Norman Moss

Our Club grows stronger each year in interest and im¬
portance to all its members. Members of Amity enjoy and
look forward to the monthly affairs at the Center, especially
their gala New Year’s Eve Party and June Banquet.
The members of the Amity Club welcome all new
couples who make our community their home. We sin¬

DAVID GOLDRING,
President
Now in its 4th year of operation, this young social
club for married couples with either spouse being under
the age of 32, completed another successful year. The
monthly affairs under the advisorship of the Vice-Presi¬
dent and a different member of the Board are in charge
along with a committee composed of the membership at
large. Each member and his wife is asked to work on one
affair per year. The highlight of our year’s activities is the
extravagant New Year's Eve Party. Another outstanding
affair this year was the trip to Daytona Beach for Jai Alai.
Our organization is open to all married couples and
anyone interested is asked to contact our membership
chairmen, Mr. and Mrs. Sy Bellack, 725-8513, or Mr. and
Mrs. Lawrence Dubow, FL 9-9054.

PORTRAIT OF A LEADER
Frank E. Diamond, C.L.U.
He did it again! By providing a great life with Gulf Life for
a great number of our area citizens, Frank E. Diamond has
again become a qualifying and repeating member of the
Million Dollar Round Table. This time it was for his 1963
performance. He also qualified for the National Quality
Award.
Frank started with Gulf Life in Atlanta in 1937 and later
headed up the Estate Planning Department of the Home
Office. He is now Manager of the Gateway Ordinary Agency
at 1639 Atlantic Boulevard—telephone 359-2778.
A recognized authority in his field, Frank has taught many
C.L.U. classes in taxation, business insurance, estate planning
and related subjects.
Mr. Diamond is past president of the local C.L.U. chapter,
and member of the Jacksonville Life Underwriters Association
and the Northwest Florida Estate Planning Council.
Gulf Life

Founded 1911

===== PAGE 164 =====
Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
ROBERT I. MARCUS
Director
President George Richter
Vice-Presidents Dr. Sam Witten,
Herbert Panken, Joe P. Safer,
Mrs. Felix Glickstein
Treasurer Aaron Block
Auditor Robert M. Reiders
Secretary Ben L. Friedman
Hon. Presidents Alexander Brest,
P. N. Coleman, Harry Gendzier, Morton
Hirschberg, Edgar Felson, A. L. Schneider
GEORGE RICHTER
President
Serving the entire Jewish community of Jacksonville, the
Jewish Community Council is the central Welfare Fund and
Community Relations body of the local Jewish community.
An association of individuals, synagogues, organizations and
social service agencies, the program encompasses key ac¬
tivities which give unity, cohesion and coordination to local
Jewish communal life.
A community-wide annual Welfare Fund Campaign pro¬
vides support to significant local, national and overseas or¬
ganizations. The National United Jewish Appeal, has ex¬
pressed American Jewry’s responsibility for the welfare and
rehabilitation needs of Jewish communities the world over
for over 20 years. River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged
and the Jewish Welfare Society are the local beneficiaries.
The Council publishes a monthly newspaper known as
the Commentator. It is the official organ of the Council and
its member organizations.
The Council conducts a leisure time activities program
which includes Youth Council programs such as college
preparation workshops, a youth news page in the Com¬
mentator, and a youth division in the annual Jewish Welfare
Fund Campaign. The Council also sponsors a community
summer Day Camp for children between the ages of 4 and 15.

River Garden Hebrew
\
Scene from a Play presented by residents of the Home.
President Arthur J. Gutman
Executive Vice-President & Chairman Admissions....Samuel J. Thier
Vice-President & Co-Chairman Admissions Frank Diamond
Vice-President & Co-Chairman Admissions Sheldon Gendzier
Vice-President & Chairman House Committee Albert Kofka

Vice-President & Chairman Residents Welfare....Mrs. Abe Horovitz

Chairman Board of Trustees & Honorary President Joe Becker
Executive Director Sidney Entman
1963-1964 was truly a “Chai” year as River Garden celebrated
its 18th year of service to the aged and infirm of Florida. The
Morris and Sarah Wolfson Pavilion opened in October of 1963 and
since then has been operating at full capacity.
The added facilities opened new vistas of activities which con¬
tinue to enrich and broaden the range of recreational and social
Home for the Aged
Arthur Gutman, President
events. The 4th Floor solarium has become a class room, a lecture
hall, a card room, and a lounge, with a magnificent view of the
river. A full size stage and large auditorium encourages River
Garden players. In their pageantry of color, song and costume, the
players have won warm acclaim for their productions.
They have proved without doubt that old age is no barrier to
a rich and active life.
All is not fun or play at River Garden. There are the ever
prevalent problems of illness so common to the aged. The healing
arts of medicine and nursing give the support necessary to maintain
life and help the aged to live to maximum capacity. Emphasis is on
“wellness” and not on “illness”.
It is this basic principle of a healthy mind in a healthy body
which dominates the way of life at River Garden.

Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society
OFFICERS
President Mrs. William Goldman
Vice-President Mrs. Roy J. Sloat
Vice-President Mrs. I. P. Edwards
Vice-President Mrs. Myron Rosenberg
Recording Secretary Mrs. William Silverman
Financial Secretary Mrs. Albert Kofka
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Sam Lazarus
Treasurer, Dues Mrs. Samuel J. Thier
Treasurer, Projects Mrs. Louis Witten
Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society was founded forty
years ago to help the needy of the Jewish faith. It was the
dream and hope of our founders to establish a Home for the
Aged in Jacksonville. This dream became a reality when
River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged was founded in
1946. Since that time our role became that of the auxiliary
to the Home.
We provide extra-budgetary needs such as furnishings,
redecorating, air conditioning, kitchen and infirmary equip¬
ment, as well as equipment for the Hobby Shop. Funds are
raised through Tombola, Donor and Social Printing proj¬
ects, in addition to annual dues and life memberships in
Ladies Aid. Linens are provided by an annual Linen Shower
which we sponsor.
The Grey Ladies Program of the Home is a Ladies Aid
project. Within the past year, this service was expanded to
include a corps of teenage Gardenettes who serve on week¬
ends and during vacation periods.
River Garden residents participate in projects served
by auxiliary members, among them being the residents’
magazine, ceramics and art classes, and other Hobby Shop
activities.
The auxiliary supplies a door-to-door transportation serv¬
ice for the weekly Golden Sunshine Club meetings held at
River Garden. This autonomous group emerged from our
OFFICERS
weekly Senior Citizens meetings. Ladies Aid representatives
continue to lend support and guidance to the group which
provides fellowship and companionship to oldsters from the
general community as well as River Garden’s own residents.
With the opening of the new Wolfson Infirmary wing,
the work and responsibilities of the auxiliary has reached a
new magnitude. Ladies Aid pledges to continue its efforts
and cooperation in bringing not only the material but also
the affectional and spiritual values so necessary for our
elder citizens.

Jewish Family and
Children's Service
OFFICERS
President
Vice-Pres
Vice-Pres
Vice-Pres
Vice-Pres

Jack Coleman
...Lewis Ansbacher

..Dr. Morris Price
Bert Whitfield

Lawrence Rackow
Lawrence Rackow, Director Jack Coleman, President
Founded in 1917, the JFCS has been assisting its clients solve
personal problems for the past 47 years. Last year some 300 different
families were served in one or more programs offered by the agency.
The monthly average came to 89.
For the sake of understanding, the services offered may be di¬
vided into 10 categories:
1. Personal Counseling
2. Adoption Program
3. Foster Home Program
4. Assistance to the Aged
5. Employment and Vocational
Services
6. New American Program
7. Short-term Financial
Assistance
8. Transient Program
9. Prison Program
10. Day Camp & College
Scholarship Program
Actually, problems frequently do not neatly fit themselves into
exclusive categories — even the broad ones suggested above. A work
problem or a parent-child problem might have ramifications that in¬
volve personal counseling, health, finances, family relations, etc. One
of the strengths of the agency is that it is so strongly client oriented,
With the help of an experienced sympathetic professionally trained
worker, the client is helped to get a clearer focus of his problem or
problems and to locate and devise means of overcoming them. Some¬
times “just talking” helps, or the resources can be found directly in
the clients or the agency. Other times the resources of the com¬
munity, region or even nation, public and private, may be required.
Here, because of the strictest respect for the rights of the clients and
the confidential nature of the relationship, the consent of the client
is always secured. The close cooperation of River Garden Hebrew
Home, the congregations, the 40 other UCS agencies, the schools,
the public and private health and welfare agencies and especially the
outstanding cooperation of the professional men — rabbis, doctors,
dentists, lawyers, etc. — makes possible the degree of success in so
many situations. Last year, in its search to constantly improve condi¬
tions and service, the agency became an agent for college loans pro¬
vided by the Children's Services of Atlanta, Ga.; worked out agree¬
ments with a number of agencies for care of unmarried mothers and
the children available for adoption, and established an independent
group of friendly visitors to the aged in their own homes.
The understanding and cooperation of the Jewish community is
vital to insure the best service. The response in terms of donations of
clothing has been magnificent but more work has to be done about
alerting the agency of available employment opportunities. A whole¬
hearted support of both the United Community Services and the
Jewish Welfare Fund drives makes this work possible!

The essential function of the Zionist Organization of
America is to keep alive in this country an interest in the
State of Israel. This interest is many-sided, as the efforts of
Zionism always were. The political objective was the creation
of a Jewish State. To this end it was necessary to revive the
Hebrew language, acquire land, train colonists for settle¬
ment and raise funds to finance the project. All this the
Zionist movement did.
Myer Cohen, President
Today, the Z.O.A. continues to foster support for the
State of Israel. Moreover, the Z.O.A. seeks to strengthen
the bonds between American Jewry and the Jewish State on
the basis of the cultural tradition that is our common
heritage.
Religious differences present no obstacle as Zionist en¬
compasses all shades of religious belief in every global area.
The Z.O.A. invites all Jewish men to join its ranks.

Senior Hadassah
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Henry D. Cristol
Vice President (Fund-Raising) Mrs. Bernard Richter
Vice-President (Program) Mrs. Maurice Goldstein
Vice-President (Membership) Mrs. David Lewis
Vice-President (Education) Mrs. Jack Shorstein
Treasurer Mrs. Arnold Hoffman
Recording Secretary Mrs. Edward Siegal
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Norman Moss
Financial Secretary Mrs. Irwin Gendzier
Hadassah was founded in 1912 by Henrietta Szold, and from
a mere handful of dedicated American women, has grown to a
membership of over 318,000.
Hadassah projects are actively functioning on two far-flung
horizons. On the American scene, an Educational Program, a Youth
Activities program, the Young Judeans and the Junior Hadassah,
Hadassah co-sponsors with Z.O.A. Camp Judaea, a summer camp in
Hendersonville, North Carolina. In Israel, Hadassah encompasses
Hadassah Medical Organization, Medical Center, Youth Aliyah,
Vocational Education, and Jewish National Fund. By helping Israel
help itself, so that it can take its place with dignity among the
freedom-loving nations of the world, Hadassah reaffirms the Jewish
faith in the heritage of freedom.
Two groups comprise the Jacksonville Chapter: the Senior
Hadassah and the Hannah Senesch Evening Group. Almost 800
women boast membership in the local chapter, and annually seek
to raise quotas totaling nearly $20,000 in order to meet commitments
largely in Israel, as well as in America.
Officers of Hadassah
Senior Hadassah meets every second Tuesday of each month
from October through May. This coming year’s calendar offers a
variety of activities with a well-rounded educational and social pro¬
gram to interest every member.
Hannah Senesch-Evening Group
of Hadassah

manitarian program, this congenial group intersperses sociability

Meetings in the Jacksonville Jewish Center are held the second
Monday of each month with an “open house” invitation to all.

Young Judaea
Young Judaea, sponsored by Hadassah and Z.O.A.,
has completed another prosperous and successful year
with three regional conventions, various religious and
social programs and an Israeli Fair at the River Garden
Home for the Aged. The Hashomerim boys also helped
with the Blue Box project.
Eight Jacksonville Judaeans attended the Southern
Region Summer Convention at Camp Judaea in Hender¬
sonville, N.C. in June. Camp Judaea in Hendersonville
is a major project of Young Judaea. Hadassah and
Z.O.A. sponsor this 4 or 8 week camp period with a
full camping and Zionist program. Camp Judaea serves
to develop in youth the ideals of American freedom,
an appreciation of the Jewish people, and ethical con¬
cepts of Judaism.
Eileen Feinberg

B'nai B’rith Women are dedicated to serving you, your family,
our country and all humanity, through our support of the following
B’nai B’rith philanthropies; National Jewish Hospital for tuberculosis
and chest diseases in Denver, Colorado; Bellefaire Children’s Home
for emotionally disturbed children in Cleveland, Ohio; Leo N. Levi
Memorial Hospital for arthritic & rheumatoid diseases in Hot Springs,
Arkansas; the B’nai B'rith Family Center for Jewish patients at the
Mayo Clinic in Rochester; and the Four Freedoms Library in Wash¬
ington, D.C.; B'nai B’rith projects in Israel include the Martyrs’
Forest, Hillel at the Hebrew University, the Veterans’ Rehabilitation
Center and the B'nai B'rith Women’s sponsored Children’s Home.
The most widely known B'nai B'rith project Anti-Defamation League
works for all Jewish people and all people of all faiths. Locally:
“The Dolls for Democracy”, distribution of memo books to the pub¬
lic schools, sponsorship of the B'nai B’rith Girls, Aid to Pine Castle
School, Hillel at the University of Florida and The Very new Spanish
Officers
Classes and English Classes at the River Garden Home for the Aged.
We also sponsor two Jewish papers daily to the River Garden Home
for the Aged.
The State Convention held in Miami was attended by Mrs.
Danglo, President and she was elected to the State Board. The Dis¬
trict Convention was attended by the State Federation of BBW, past
president Mrs. Jack Dorman and the chapters encumbent President.
This coming year we will have several changes in this chapters
activities; The “Calendar Ad-Date Book” which promises a new
vista in the financial income. The newly formed B’nai B’rith
Women’s Bowling League. To keep our youth informed and active
we the women of B'nai B’rith find that the closeness of the parent
organization with the BBG’s affords us the opportunity and awarded
our President by being made an honorary member.
All this proves that the name B'nai B’rith is synonymous with
our Motto “SERVICE: FIDELITY: DEVOTION.”

AZA can proudly boast of accomplishing one of the most sought for projects, that of
“OPERATION AMIGO,” a project which brought 30 Guatemalan students to Jacksonville
for two weeks. Through the efforts of Bruce Gibbs and Louise Hilsenrad, along with Engle¬
wood High School and the B'nai B'rith members who attended Englewood.
We held an interfaith program and a study of Judaism. Gators BBYO were host to the
North Florida Convention at which time Jeff Bootzin was elected as North Florida Council
President. The discussion groups subjects pertaining to the Jewish Youth, World Problems
and Domestic Problems were very successful.
Our members participated in several cookie sales.
Our thanks to Myron Blattner, who again this year gave of his time with endless patience.

“Gators" BBG is proud to say that it has had a very successful year. “Gators'5 started
the year with an invitational tea for prospective members. We then collected candy on
Halloween for the children in Brewster and Hope Haven Hospitals. We received many
honors at our Regional BBYO convention (all of Florida) in December. We were awarded
“Most Spirited" of all the chapters, our scrapbook won first place, our Oratory won second
place, and our Alma Mater won first place. BBG has had two successful Brownie Sales in
which we collected over one-hundred dollars. Last month we presented a program for the
residents at River Garden. We concluded our official year with an Installation Luncheon for
the newly elected officers at the Town House Restaurant. Superlatives were presented and
new officers installed.

The National Council of Jewish Women is an organization dedicated to
furthering human welfare in the Jewish and general communities, locally, nationally,
and internationally.
Some of our community projects are:
Teachers’ Aide — helping elementary school children with
problems with their academic work;
Juvenile Shelter — a program of recreational activity the
year-round conducted by our volunteers;
Medical Loan Shelf — supplies hospital equipment to indi¬
gents of the community through the Visiting Nurses Assn.;
Mental Health Fair — educational program for the com¬
munity that is co-sponsored with the Mental Health Assn.;
Sewing Committee—makes baby garments for needy mothers
and this year sent a package to the Alaskan Disaster;
Mrs. Sol Goldman,
hearing and speech President
Tribute Fund — purchases hearing aids for indigent
children;
Ship-a-Box — sends supplies and toys to kindergartens in
Israel.
The Jacksonville Section this year will observe its 25th.
Anniversary of community, state, national and inter¬
national service.

The Progressive Credit Union sponsored by the Jewish
Progressive Society is organized to encourage thrift and pro¬
vide a safe, convenient medium whereby members can invest
and accumulate savings. The Credit Union carries life sav¬
ings insurance recently increased to $2,000 and loan protec¬
tion insurance without any direct cost to the members. Last
year the Board declared a 3%% dividend on savings and
10% rebate of interest to borrowing members.
Ten Commandments of our Credit Union: The Member
is the most important person in our Credit Union. The
Member is not an outsider—he is an owner of our Credit
Union. The Member is not an interruption of our work—he
is the purpose of it. The Member does us a favor when he

Our Day Camp Six
Our youngest group, the Pee Wees, composed of four
year old boys and girls with head Counselor Barbara
Goldstein.
Our Tadpoles made up of boys and girls going into
the first grade with their head Counselors Gail Grossman
and Richard Kidder.
Our Midgets with their head Counselors Marvin Slott
and Anita Mesnick who supervised the seven and eight
year old boys and girls.
Our Juniors who make up one of the most active
groups at the Camp are composed of nine and ten year
olds with head Counselors Jerry Cohn, Lane Berkwit and
Judy Horovitz.
Our oldest campers known as the Senior boys and girls
(ages 11 to 13), with head Counselors Jack Fleet, Karen
Caslar and Rozie Goldstein.
Counselors-in-Training class with their Director Mrs.
Robert Fischer. This group of girls and boys is composed
of 14 year olds going into the 9th grade who are receiving
training for future positions on our staff.

PURIM (literally, “lots”) — this gayest of Jewish holidays is nonetheless
named for the lots cast before Haman to select a day of execution for the
Jews. On the fateful day, however, it was the wicked Haman who
perished — thanks to Queen Esther and her cousin, Mordecai. The full
story is told in the Megillah (Book of Esther, our symbol) which, on this
day, is read aloud twice in the synagogue. During the reading, children
twirl their gragers (noisemakers) whenever Haman is mentioned, to drown
out his name.

PESAH (Passover), the Feast of Unleavened Bread—an eight-day celebra¬
tion of the deliverance of the Jews from Egyptian slavery. During the tra¬
ditional Seder meal, on the first two nights of Passover, Jewish families
read the Haggadah (a ritual recitation of the story of the Exodus).
Throughout the Passover periods, foods are selected according to special
dietary laws. Instead of bread, Jews eat matzoh (unleavened bread) to
recall the hasty flight from Egypt, when there was no time to allow the
bread to rise.

THE HORIZON OF JEWISH LIFE in Jacksonville
was raised symbolically Sunday May 31 when a Mezuzah was
placed on the entrance to the rouse on a new site of land on
State Road 13. This 36 acre property purchased in November
1963, will provide, at the conclusion of an extended, many-
phased construction plan, a new home for the elaborate pro¬
gram of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. The Shaharit service
that Sunday morning, out of doors, under the great oak that
is already being called the “prayer tree”, was the first public
worship service conducted at the new site.
The broad scope of activity conducted this summer at
the new site initiated a new dimension in congregational life,
which, when added to the existing year-round schedule at the
Center will allow for the complete fulfillment of the highest
ambitions of a Synagogue Center.
The chronological history of the Center dates back to
the founding of Congregation B’nai Israel in 1901. At the
time of its incorporation in 1903, the membership had grown
from five to forty families. Acquisition of the first building
was in 1907 when membership reached 75 families. In 1927
Congregation B’nai Israel became the Jacksonville Jewish
Center, a Conservative Synagogue affiliated with the United
Synagogue of America, and changed its location to the present
site at Third and Silver streets, a landmark in Springfield
for almost four decades.
To serve the growing needs of the youth of the com¬
munity, the Activities building was started in 1950 and was
dedicated the following year. Plans were made for the com¬
plete renovation of the Sanctuary, which was completed in
1958. A new school building was finished and added to the
plant in 1959.
The real history of the Center is seen in the panorama
of generations and events over more than three score of
years. In wartime and peacetime, in depression and in pros¬
perity, in sorrow and in great joy, a dynamic and forth-right
religious response to the needs of Jewish people has been the
purpose and the achievement of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center.
Guided by the standards and concepts of the Conservative
movement and directed toward the overall religious ideal of
bringing American Jews closer to relevant Judaism on every
level of experience, the Center iinds itself now propelled with
a fresh thrust that will carry it forward to a new position of
leadership in JACKSONVILLE AND IN THE COUNTRY.
The Olympic A.A.U. Pool during construction as seen in April 1964.
Our completed Olympic Pool as shown on opening day, with Jim Clem¬
ents, Architect; Jack and Sam Price, Co-Chairmen of the Construction
Committee; Robert Fischer, Executive Director; and Herman Weiss,
member of Board of Trustees.
The completed 25 ft. circular wading pool with showerhead, being
enjoyed by youngsters at the dedication services on May 31.
The present two story, 13 room house situated at the front entrance of
our 36 acre site.
